Cindy Wells, who was on welfare when she arrived in Madison eight years ago, has prospered there. ``I became a whole person,'' she said. (Morry Gash/New York Times Photo.)  (NYT1) PRINCETON, N.J. -- May 7, 1995 -- RUGBY-WOMEN, 5-7 -- The Penn State and Princeton women's rugby teams fighting for the ball Sunday during their national collegiate championship game. Princeton's Kim Henderson, top center, was caught in the scrum. About 170 universities now field women's teams on the club level. The physical nature of the game, women say, has boosted their confidence and assertiveness and heightened their sense of teamwork and responsibility. (Laura Pedrick/New York Times Photo.) The copy is for use by NYTimes News Service clients, AMs and PMs, of July 16 and thereafter.  The package includes nine stories of national interest as well as 55 profiles of Olympians from the Los Angeles area. A number of these Olympians compete for L.A. area colleges and are from throughout the country. A slugline note will be included in such instances.  Inch counts are provided.  A budget will follow. OLY-ATLANTA (Undated) _ The city of Atlanta seemed to be moving at a comfortable pace, on target for the July 19 opening of the Summer Games. Suddenly, however, things have become a bit more frentic. 20 inches.  By Jon Wilner. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-VBB-BEACH (Los Angeles) _ The first U.S. Olympic beach volleyball contingent isn't made up of a bunch of folks who necessarily like each other. But they can win medals. 20 inches.  By Joe Jares. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-BKO-DREAM (Undated) _ Set against the thousands of amateurs who have trained their whole lives for the privilege of melting in the Georgia sun are the dozen pros who won't have to work hard at all to win gold. What could be more American, more '90s, than another Olympic Dream Team? Ignore that question, or the potential of this 12-man gang, and you risk missing some of the most compelling stories of the Summer Games. 23 inches.  By Marc Stein. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-MEDIA (Los Angeles) _ Navigating through the humid drama of athletic competition in that sweatbox known as Atlanta between July 22 and Aug. 4 takes more than a stack of TV listings and a good anti-persperent. It takes knowledge. Here's what to expect, what not to get your hopes up for, and other shortcuts for 17 days of coverage by NBC Sports. 27 inches.  By Tom Hoffarth. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-SPTBYSPT (Undated) _ A sport-by-sport overview of the Games, explaining who's the favorite for each medal. 74 inches.  By Jon Wilner. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-HISTORY (Undated) _ They were supposed to be the 1996 Athens Games. What better place to celebrate the Centennial Olympics than the capital of their homeland and the site of their rebirth? But on September 18, 1990, in a Tokyo hotel ballroom, the International Olympic Committee voted 51-35 to award the Centennial Games to Atlanta, a city known worldwide as the backdrop for ``Gone With the Wind.'' We look back at the 100 years of the Olympics. 30 inches.  By Jon Wilner. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-OVERVIEW (Undated) _ Atlanta's Olympic dream becomes reality Friday, when the Games of the XXVI Olympiad begin with the Opening Ceremony at the new, 83,000-seat Olympic Stadium. Billy Payne, president of the Atlanta Committee for the Olympic Games, has all the ingredients in place for an historic 17 days. Every nation that received an invitation accepted _ essentially, the world boycotted a boycott _ and that translates into Fifteen nations once under the auspices of the Soviet Union will participate. Five competitors used to be a part of Yugoslavia. Former pariah South Africa is here, as is America's antagonist, Cuba. 24 inches.  By Jon Wilner. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-VENTRE-COLUMN (Undated) _ Do you know what's really bad about the Olympics? It's that we no longer have those commies to hate. So we need somebody new. 24 inches.  By Michael Ventre. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-VBL-INDOOR (Los Angeles) _ Both the men's and women's volleyball teams have excellent chances to medal. 25 inches.  By Joe Jares. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-BKO-DIVAC (Los Angeles) _ As a seven-year NBA veteran, he knows maybe better than anyone in the Olympic men's basketball tournament that the Dreamers III will scarcely be challenged, much less beaten. Divac is required by unofficial Olympic law to be optimistic, but in private he'll own up to reality and admit that Yugoslavia is playing for silver. This summer, like this coming season, gold won't be Divac's color. 13 inches.  By Marc Stein. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-RUN-ACUFF (Los Angeles) _ Amy Acuff knows that some people don't take her seriously when she says it. That's OK, though. The UCLA high jumper admits she has set a lofty goal for herself, a goal she isn't compelled to keep to herself. It'll happen, though. One day, she is certain, she will prove her doubters wrong with a single leap. 15.9 inches.  By Michael Rosenthal. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-HKY-AMAR (Camarillo, Calif.) _ Camarillo's Larry Amar is the United States' best field hockey player. Now 24, Amar is the national team captain and a two-time U.S. Field Hockey Association Player of the Year. Teammates call him the Golden Boy. ``The ball always seems to bounce my way,'' Amar said. ``That's kind of funny.'' 12.7 inches.  By Kevin Acee. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-RUN-BENITEZ (Woodland Hills, Calif.) _ Quincy Watts failed to earn a berth on the U.S. Olympic team. Taft High School's track and field team, though, will be represented in the Olympics. Ruben Benitez, a teammate of Watts at Taft, will run for El Salvador in the 100 and 200 meters in Atlanta. 15.5 inches.  By Kirby Lee. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file.)  OLY-RUN-BOLDON (Los Angeles) _ You may not have heard of him, but Ato Boldon is a star athlete. At least in his native country, Trinidad, for whom the UCLA senior will compete in the Olympic games. In June, he posted a time of 9.92 seconds in the 100 meters at the NCAA Track and Field Championships in Eugene, Ore., the fastest clocking by anyone this year. He thinks his chances are good, saying, ``Right now, I'd have to be the favorite in the 100.'' 17 inches.  By Michael Ventre. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-SWM-BRIDGEWATER (Los Angeles) _ The Olympic experience is something Brad Bridgewater has been anticipating for four years now. As an 18-year-old freshman at the University of Texas, he was invited to the 1992 Olympic Trials. ``I was frightened by the whole thing,'' said Bridgewater, a two-time Florida state swimming champion. ``I was just a kid. It was pretty incredible. I was swimming with the best in the country. I was pretty satisfied with it.'' Four years changed Bridgewater, who swallowed the tough experience of finishing seventh in the 200-meter backstroke in 1992 and used it for inspiration for this year's trials. This time, the 6-foot-4 Bridgewater, now from USC, blew the field away and landed a berth in the 200-meter backstroke. 14.4 inches.  By Gary Washburn. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-HKY-BUTCHER (Simi Valley, Calif.) _ Nick Butcher of Simi Valley is, at age 20, the youngest member of the U.S. Olympic field hockey team. He was a star soccer player at Royal High School but turned down numerous college scholarships to pursue his Olympic dream. Nick is being groomed for international greatness down the line but he can contribute now. 15 inches.  By Eric Noland. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-VBL-CASTRO (La Crescenta, Calif.) _ After Gail Castro and her partner, Deb Richardson, surprised the beach-volleyball world by fighting through the trials in Baltimore and making the Olympics, they were called before the TV cameras for the customary quick interview. It was Castro's chance to say, ``Hey, a 38-year-old wife and mom has just defied the odds and made it to Atlanta!'' Or, ``Dreams do come true!'' Or maybe just, ``Hi, Larry! Hi, Larry Jr.!'' Instead, she opened her mouth and _ nothing. A chatty, friendly woman who had just showed guts and grit to help win a vital match, she froze as if the microphone were emitting tonsil-paralyzing gas. Partner Richardson had to do all the talking. Castro (nee Wolze) _ a former star at Crescenta Valley High, Valley College and Long Beach State, an ex-indoor pro in Italy, a pioneer of the women's pro-beach tour _ had all her struggles and disappointments and broken beach partnerships and sacrifices flash through her mind. 15.2 inches.  By Joe Jares. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-ROW-COLLINS (Thousand Oaks, Calif.) _ David Collins of Thousand Oaks has sacrificed plenty to compete in one of rowing's most grueling events _ lightweight fours without coxswain. ``When you get to the end (of a 2,000-meter, nearly six-minute race), I mean, your legs are burning, your lungs are screaming, and your vision starts to become a tunnel,'' the 26-year-old Westlake High graduate said. ``You focus on what's ahead _ the last 20 strokes, the person in front of you _ and almost let your body take over because your mind wants to tell you to stop.'' 15.9 inches.  By Kevin Modesti. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-SOF-CORNELL (Woodland Hills, Calif.) _ Woodland Hills' Sheila Cornell, at 34, is one of the United States' most distinguished softball players. She is a 13-year veteran of the U.S. national team, has won eight gold medals in international competition with the United States and is a 10-time Amateur Softball Association All-American. The 1980 Taft High School graduate is also one of the best hitters in the game. ``She's the person I would most like to have at bat with the game on the line,'' said Olympic team coach Ralph Weekly.  By Kevin Acee. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file). (His wife Keisha was due on the 13th). ``I thank God for these two joyous events in one moment,'' the USC graduate says in the driveway of his Valencia home. ``They balance each other out. OLY-SOC-FAWCETT (Los Angeles) _ Joy Fawcett did not play soccer because she hoped one day she would represent her country at the Olympics or the World Cup. That's because when Fawcett began, there was no women's soccer in the Olympics or World Cup. ``We were doing it because we loved the sport,'' Fawcett said. ``It's fun to see it grow.'' The 28-year-old Fawcett has seen many firsts for women's soccer. In addition to being a member of the U.S. National Team that won the first World Cup for women, she became the first women's soccer coach at UCLA. Now, she is competing in the first Olympics where women's soccer is a medal sport. 13.3 inches.  By Kathy Orton. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-HDB-FERCHO (Camarillo, Calif.) _ Denny Fercho plays team handball, a game not many Americans know. It's NOT like racquetball; rather, it resembles a combination of basketball, hockey, soccer, water polo and football. Fercho was exposed to the game first when he saw a match at the '84 Olympics in L.A. and, with the help of a grant of the Amateur Athletics Foundation, soon mastered it and became one of America's best players. Then he was cut from the team, told he was too small. He overcame that obstacle by mastering the fundamentals better than anyone. Says U.S. coach Rick Oleksyk: ``Denny is the smallest in size on the team, but possesses the largest heart. His desire is second to none.'' 14.8 inches.  By Kevin Modesti. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-SOF-FERNANDEZ (Los Angeles) _ If U.S. Olympic team pitcher/infielder Lisa Fernandez is not already the best softball player in the nation, just wait a while. ``God didn't give me the talent to run fast or be quick or be strong,'' she said, ``but He gave me determination.'' That quality has carried Fernandez from an inauspicious beginning to a level of play unmatched in either softball or baseball, in the opinion of UCLA coach and softball Hall of Famer Sharron Backus. ``Lisa Fernandez is the best all-around softball player in the history of the sport,'' said Backus, who coached Fernandez for four years at UCLA. ``If you try to compare her to a major-league baseball player, she has no equal. There is no one who can pitch, hit and field like Lisa in the major leagues, nor has there been an equal in collegiate softball.'' 14.3 inches.  By Dave Shelburne. OLY-ROW-FULLER (Westlake Village, Calif.) _ When the Fuller family first moved to the shores of Thousand Oaks' Lake Sherwood 20 years ago, one of the first gifts 8-year-old Amy received was a 12-foot wooden row boat. ``Her brother had a (sailboat),'' Diane Fuller, Amy's mother, remembered recently. ``She would get out there and challenge him to a race around the island in the middle of the lake.'' Thus began a sporting endeavor that brought Fuller a silver medal in the Barcelona Olympics in 1992, an accomplishment she hopes to improve on as her competitive life winds to a close this week in Atlanta. The odds are good that is precisely what will happen. Fuller and her world-champion American teammates are the favorites to win the women's eights class in Olympic rowing. 11.8 inches. Godina now wants to strive for goals others have thought impossible. Foremost is becoming one of the best in the world in both throws. OLY-RUN-HERAZO (Los Angeles) _ Her sport is a walking contradiction, but there is universal agreement on Victoria Herazo's designation as a world-class athlete. It's just hard to get recognition when your Olympic event seems based on compromise, or at least, appears slightly confused. But the Castaic resident has gotten well past all the jokes reserved for racewalking, a sport that is a cross between walking and running, but strays from completely relying on either motion. A simple rule to remember is that racewalkers are not allowed to lift both feet off the ground simultaneously during a race. 18.5 inches.  By Scott Wolf. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-ARC-HUISH (Simi Valley, Calif.) _ Justin Huish of Simi Valley, with grungy hipness and athletic good looks, has become the cover boy of sorts of the U.S. archery team. If you drive down Broken Arrow Street in Simi, you might come across the 21-year-old. To practice for the Olympics _ when he's not at the national training center in San Diego _ he shoots at a target set up between the barbecue and the wind chimes in his parents' backhard. To reach the minimum international range of 30 meters, he backs up through the garage, down the driveway and onto the street named, quite coincidentally, Broken Arrow. Then he fires away _ through a narrow doorway and, sometimes, over a sleeping Akita named Bear. 14.3 inches.  By Kevin Modesti. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-BBO-JONES (Los Angeles) _ With all of his physical skills, Jacque Jones looks deep inside when asked about his true strength. The former USC baseball player and second-round draft pick of the Minnesota Twins is as strong mentally as he is physically. While coaches and scouts rave about his rare combination of speed and power, Jones concentrates on his mental game. The outfielder credits a key adjustment in his early years at USC for his success on the U.S. national team. ``At first, everything that went wrong, I got down on myself,'' Jones said from Houston, Texas. ``When I got to USC, Coach (Mike) Gillespie taught me to keep my head up and stay in the game. Ever since then, it's been my strength.'' 14.1 inches.  By Gary Washburn. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file). Ultimately, she developed into one of the state's top throwers and earned a scholarship to UCLA, where she has competed in the shot put and discus in the NCAA meet the past two years. She has twice earned All-American honors indoors. Kawar, a resident of La Crescenta since her parents brought the family to the United States in 1989, will represent Jordan in the Olympics. ``They got a little excited,'' said Kawar, one of the top weight throwers at UCLA. It's no wonder. Until Kawar came along, she said, the national record in the shot put ``was around 37 feet.'' Now, her best mark of 55 feet, } inches has been certified as the best ever in the country's modest track and field program. (She has thrown 183-11 in the discus). Suddenly, Jordan, where she already has become something of a national figure, had an American-trained, world-class weight thrower. 15.2 inches.  By Michael Rosenthal. Miller, a graduate of USC, is the daughter of Lennox Miller, the sprinter from Jamaica who won a silver medal in the 100-meter dash in the 1968 Olympics and a bronze in the same event in 1972. The first time she stepped on the track, as a child, she displayed raw speed handed down by her father, speed that enabled to succeed at Muir High School and USC. However, chronic injuries prevented her from taking that one final step to stardom _ until now. Miller, 24, outleaned Gwen Torrence to earn the third and final Olympic berth in the 200 and, as the fourth-place finisher in the 100, will be part of the 400 relay pool. 14.5 inches. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-SWIM-SALMEEN (Los Angeles) _ Annette Salmeen was a strong swimmer in high school, but she never thought she would make it to the Olympics. Her scientific approach to life _ she's a chemistry major at UCLA _ has helped her perfect her swimming, and she will be the first female Bruin since 1972 to compete in the Olympics, in the 200 butterfly and 800 free relay. ``Annette understands the difference between weight and buoyancy,'' says Bruins assistant coach Brad Burnham. ``She knows what a vector is. She understands the difference between speed and velocity and the weight of your hips versus the mass of your hips. Some swimmers go by what it feels like in the water. Annette needs numbers.'' To solve one of her recurring problems _ a slow-moving center of mass _ Salmeen watched underwater video and worked with her coach to figure out a way to position her body parts in the most effective way possible. ``To Annette, swimming fast is a problem that must be solved,'' Burnham said, ``and the pool is like her labratory.'' 14.7 inches. At the relatively advanced age of 29, he ran personal bests of 10.19 in the 100 and 20.18 in the 200. He capped his splendid year by placing third in the 200 in the world championships. 14 inches.  By Jon Wilner. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-HKY-SWILLIAMS (Newbury Park, Calif.) _ Scott Williams vividly remembers the first time he ever heard of field hockey. It was the year before the 1984 Olympics in Los Angeles and members of the U.S. Olympic team were giving 12-year-old Williams and his classmates a demonstration of their game. Mike Newton and other '84 Olympians had shown up that day at Newbury Park's Sequoia Junior High School to drum up interest for a fledgling youth program. The sport already enjoyed growing enthusiasm among adults in Ventura County. ``They told us if we liked it to come out Saturday and sign up and they'd show us how to play,'' said Williams, who has learned the game better than anyone might have predicted. Thirteen years later, he is still playing _ as a teammate of Newton on the U.S. Olympic squad that will compete this month in the Atlanta Games. 16.2 inches.  By Dave Shelburne. OLY-SWM-BRIDGEWATER (Los Angeles) _ The Olympic experience is something Brad Bridgewater has been anticipating for four years now. As an 18-year-old freshman at the University of Texas, he was invited to the 1992 Olympic Trials. ``I was frightened by the whole thing,'' said Bridgewater, a two-time Florida state swimming champion. ``I was just a kid. It was pretty incredible. I was swimming with the best in the country. I was pretty satisfied with it.'' Four years changed Bridgewater, who swallowed the tough experience of finishing seventh in the 200-meter backstroke in 1992 and used it for inspiration for this year's trials. This time, the 6-foot-4 Bridgewater, now from USC, blew the field away and landed a berth in the 200-meter backstroke. 14.4 inches.  By Gary Washburn. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file). OLY-VBL-CASTRO (La Crescenta, Calif.) _ After Gail Castro and her partner, Deb Richardson, surprised the beach-volleyball world by fighting through the trials in Baltimore and making the Olympics, they were called before the TV cameras for the customary quick interview. It was Castro's chance to say, ``Hey, a 38-year-old wife and mom has just defied the odds and made it to Atlanta!'' Or, ``Dreams do come true!'' Or maybe just, ``Hi, Larry! Hi, Larry Jr.!'' Instead, she opened her mouth and _ nothing. A chatty, friendly woman who had just showed guts and grit to help win a vital match, she froze as if the microphone were emitting tonsil-paralyzing gas. Partner Richardson had to do all the talking. Castro (nee Wolze) _ a former star at Crescenta Valley High, Valley College and Long Beach State, an ex-indoor pro in Italy, a pioneer of the women's pro-beach tour _ had all her struggles and disappointments and broken beach partnerships and sacrifices flash through her mind. 15.2 inches.  By Joe Jares. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-ROW-COLLINS (Thousand Oaks, Calif.) _ David Collins of Thousand Oaks has sacrificed plenty to compete in one of rowing's most grueling events _ lightweight fours without coxswain. ``When you get to the end (of a 2,000-meter, nearly six-minute race), I mean, your legs are burning, your lungs are screaming, and your vision starts to become a tunnel,'' the 26-year-old Westlake High graduate said. ``You focus on what's ahead _ the last 20 strokes, the person in front of you _ and almost let your body take over because your mind wants to tell you to stop.'' 15.9 inches. OLY-SOF-CORNELL (Woodland Hills, Calif.) _ Woodland Hills' Sheila Cornell, at 34, is one of the United States' most distinguished softball players. The 1980 Taft High School graduate is also one of the best hitters in the game. ``She's the person I would most like to have at bat with the game on the line,'' said Olympic team coach Ralph Weekly. OLY-SOC-FAWCETT (Los Angeles) _ Joy Fawcett did not play soccer because she hoped one day she would represent her country at the Olympics or the World Cup. The 28-year-old Fawcett has seen many firsts for women's soccer. OLY-HDB-FERCHO (Camarillo, Calif.) _ Denny Fercho plays team handball, a game not many Americans know. It's NOT like racquetball; rather, it resembles a combination of basketball, hockey, soccer, water polo and football. Fercho was exposed to the game first when he saw a match at the '84 Olympics in L.A. and, with the help of a grant of the Amateur Athletics Foundation, soon mastered it and became one of America's best players. Then he was cut from the team, told he was too small. He overcame that obstacle by mastering the fundamentals better than anyone. Says U.S. coach Rick Oleksyk: ``Denny is the smallest in size on the team, but possesses the largest heart. His desire is second to none.'' 14.8 inches.  By Kevin Modesti. OLY-SOF-FERNANDEZ (Los Angeles) _ If U.S. Olympic team pitcher/infielder Lisa Fernandez is not already the best softball player in the nation, just wait a while. ``God didn't give me the talent to run fast or be quick or be strong,'' she said, ``but He gave me determination.'' That quality has carried Fernandez from an inauspicious beginning to a level of play unmatched in either softball or baseball, in the opinion of UCLA coach and softball Hall of Famer Sharron Backus. ``Lisa Fernandez is the best all-around softball player in the history of the sport,'' said Backus, who coached Fernandez for four years at UCLA. ``If you try to compare her to a major-league baseball player, she has no equal. There is no one who can pitch, hit and field like Lisa in the major leagues, nor has there been an equal in collegiate softball.'' 14.3 inches.  By Dave Shelburne. OLY-ROW-FULLER (Westlake Village, Calif.) _ When the Fuller family first moved to the shores of Thousand Oaks' Lake Sherwood 20 years ago, one of the first gifts 8-year-old Amy received was a 12-foot wooden row boat. ``Her brother had a (sailboat),'' Diane Fuller, Amy's mother, remembered recently. ``She would get out there and challenge him to a race around the island in the middle of the lake.'' Thus began a sporting endeavor that brought Fuller a silver medal in the Barcelona Olympics in 1992, an accomplishment she hopes to improve on as her competitive life winds to a close this week in Atlanta. OLY-RUN-HERAZO (Los Angeles) _ Her sport is a walking contradiction, but there is universal agreement on Victoria Herazo's designation as a world-class athlete. But the Castaic resident has gotten well past all the jokes reserved for racewalking, a sport that is a cross between walking and running, but strays from completely relying on either motion. OLY-ARC-HUISH (Simi Valley, Calif.) _ Justin Huish of Simi Valley, with grungy hipness and athletic good looks, has become the cover boy of sorts of the U.S. archery team. If you drive down Broken Arrow Street in Simi, you might come across the 21-year-old. To practice for the Olympics _ when he's not at the national training center in San Diego _ he shoots at a target set up between the barbecue and the wind chimes in his parents' backhard. To reach the minimum international range of 30 meters, he backs up through the garage, down the driveway and onto the street named, quite coincidentally, Broken Arrow. Then he fires away _ through a narrow doorway and, sometimes, over a sleeping Akita named Bear. 14.3 inches.  By Kevin Modesti. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-BBO-JONES (Los Angeles) _ With all of his physical skills, Jacque Jones looks deep inside when asked about his true strength. The former USC baseball player and second-round draft pick of the Minnesota Twins is as strong mentally as he is physically. While coaches and scouts rave about his rare combination of speed and power, Jones concentrates on his mental game. The outfielder credits a key adjustment in his early years at USC for his success on the U.S. national team. ``At first, everything that went wrong, I got down on myself,'' Jones said from Houston, Texas. ``When I got to USC, Coach (Mike) Gillespie taught me to keep my head up and stay in the game. Ever since then, it's been my strength.'' 14.1 inches.  By Gary Washburn. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-RUN-KAWAR (La Crescenta, Calif.) _ At Crescenta Valley High School, Nada Kawar played basketball and, when she needed a physical education class, decided to give track and field a try. She has twice earned All-American honors indoors. Kawar, a resident of La Crescenta since her parents brought the family to the United States in 1989, will represent Jordan in the Olympics. ``They got a little excited,'' said Kawar, one of the top weight throwers at UCLA. Now, her best mark of 55 feet, } inches has been certified as the best ever in the country's modest track and field program. (She has thrown 183-11 in the discus). Suddenly, Jordan, where she already has become something of a national figure, had an American-trained, world-class weight thrower. 15.2 inches.  By Michael Rosenthal. OLY-BBO-PARQUE (Los Angeles) _ When UCLA left-handed pitcher Jim Parque was finally extended an invitation to try out for the U.S. Olympic baseball team last May, his reply was, ``What took you so long?'' ``I just knew all along I could compete with any of these guys,'' Parque said. ``I was a little disappointed with the late invitation, but an invite is an invite.'' Parque proved he deserved the tryout, because he made the elite 20-man team as a relief pitcher. The former Crescenta Valley High School star was considered a longshot at the start of training camp last June, but he showed he could be a left-handed setup man and spot starter.  By Eric Sondheimer. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-RUN-POWELL (Los Angeles) _ UCLA's Suzy Powell does not expect to win the discus in Atlanta. She is just a sophomore in college, two years removed from Downey High School in Modesto. But in a few short months, Powell, the prep all-time national leader in the event, has reached an elite level. 8.7 inches.  By Danny Schumacher. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-SWM-QUANCE (Northridge, Calif.) _ Kristine Quance won't be compteting in her signature event, the 400-meter IM, in the Atlanta Games, but don't count her out of medal contention just yet. After being disqualified by a turn judge's controversial call in the U.S. Olympic Trials in that event, the USC swimmer and Northridge native has a renewed commitment to winning the 200 IM and the 100 breast, the events for which she did qualify. Without the pressure of being the favorite, her coach thinks she had an excellent shot at a medal. 16.3 inches.  By Michael Ventre. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file.)  OLY-BKO-REGGIE (Undated) _ After a rivalry spanning three decades, this is the summer that Reggie Miller gets even with sister Cheryl. OK, maybe not. ``I will never be on the same level,'' claims Reggie, the former UCLA sharpshooter who brings his 3-point prowess to Dream Team III for the Atlanta Olympics. 8 inches.  By Marc Stein. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-SOF-RICHARDSON (Los Angeles) _ Anything would be harder for Sherman Oaks' Dot Richardson than softball, a sport she has excelled at since age 10 _ when she was recruited by a women's Class A fastpitch team in Florida. Three years later, she was playing at the highest amateur level in the nation. By age 17, Richardson was competing against the best in the world as a member of her first of 18 consecutive U.S. national teams. Richardson later helped lead UCLA to the 1982 NCAA championship. Now she's an M.D., on one-year leave from USC Medical Center, competing in the Olympics for the first time. 17 inches.  By Dave Shelburne. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-WTP-ROUSSEAU (Los Angeles) _ Of the four basic old-world substances _ earth, air, fire and water _ Alex Rousseau finds three to be irrelevant. ``I'm definitely a water-oriented person,'' said Rousseau. ``Swimming, surfing, water polo ... I love the water.'' He also excels in it. Since his introduction to water polo as a freshman at Santa Monica High School, the former UCLA star, 28, has matured into a starter on the U.S. Olympic team. 12.8 inches.  By Bill Schlotter. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-SWIM-SALMEEN (Los Angeles) _ Annette Salmeen was a strong swimmer in high school, but she never thought she would make it to the Olympics. Her scientific approach to life _ she's a chemistry major at UCLA _ has helped her perfect her swimming, and she will be the first female Bruin since 1972 to compete in the Olympics, in the 200 butterfly and 800 free relay. ``Annette understands the difference between weight and buoyancy,'' says Bruins assistant coach Brad Burnham. ``She knows what a vector is. She understands the difference between speed and velocity and the weight of your hips versus the mass of your hips. Some swimmers go by what it feels like in the water. Annette needs numbers.'' To solve one of her recurring problems _ a slow-moving center of mass _ Salmeen watched underwater video and worked with her coach to figure out a way to position her body parts in the most effective way possible. ``To Annette, swimming fast is a problem that must be solved,'' Burnham said, ``and the pool is like her labratory.'' 14.7 inches.  By Jon Wilner. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-SOC-SNITKO (Los Angeles) _ Had be been composed of less-stern stuff, Chris Snitko might have wilted in the face of the legacy. When he stepped into goal for the UCLA soccer team three years ago, it was on the heels of a long procession of glittery All-American goalies: Tim Harris, Anton Nistl, Brad Friedel. Snitko more than held his own. He kept the line of succession strong by earning All-American recognition after the 1995 season, earned some distinction in the Bruins record book, and has capped the achievements with inclusion on the U.S. Olympic men's soccer team. 14 inches.  By Eric Noland. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-RUN-TERRY (Los Angeles) _ UCLA's Mike Terry is no world-class athlete. He is barely knocking on the national-class door. He didn't qualify for the NCAA Championships. And according to one of his coaches, he would have had a very difficult time qualifying at the U.S. Olympic Trials held in Atlanta last month. But he will compete at the Summer Olympics in the 400 meters nevertheless. He will represent his father's homeland _ Antigua and Barbuda, the small island gems of the Carribean adorned with 365 beaches _ one for each day of the year. 14.1 inches.  By P.K. Daniel. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-GYM-UMPHREY (Los Angeles) _ It might be hard to find too many athletes at this year's Olympics who appreciate the opportunity more than gymnast Chainey Umphrey. Four years ago, at the Olympic Trials in Baltimore, the UCLA graduate felt like an Olympian for a teasingly brief period of time. As the gymnasts jockeyed for the seven qualifying spots, an assistant coach told Umphrey he was headed for Barcelona. By a small margin, the coach had made a huge mistake _ Umphrey missed qualifying by eight one-hundredths of a point. That was the last he wanted to hear about Barcelona. 17.4 inches.  By Brad Nelson. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-HKY-VANO (Simi Valley, Calif.) _ Tom Vano of Simi Valley is competing with Steve Wagner to be the U.S. field hockey team's goalie. Both men have had their turns surging in that competition, and although it is not certain who will start in the Olympics, Vano said that's not important. ``What's important is the team,'' he said. ```The big satisfaction comes when you put on your jersey for your country.'' 15 inches.  By Dave Shelburne. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-BOX-VARGAS (Oxnard, Calif.) _ One way or another, Fernando Vargas was going to fight. Even as an elementary school-aged child, he said, ``I loved to fight.'' And he did. In time, it was no longer news when Vargas was suspended from school, apparent evidence the boy was headed in a self-destructive direction. And then, when he was only 10, came a turning point. ``I was watching a local amateur boxing tournament on cable TV and was just mesmerized,'' said Vargas, a resident of Oxnard. ``I didn't know there was such a thing for kids. So all by myself, without my mom knowing, I found out where the gym was and went there. ``My mom didn't like it at all when she found out. She thought I would get hurt. When she saw I was hurting other people, though, I guess it was all right.'' 14.0 inches.  By Michael Rosenthal. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-TTN-WANG (Pasadena, Calif.) _ Once before, Wei Wang walked away from a career as a national-caliber table tennis player. Now, more than 15 years later, she has proven that she still has what it takes. Wang, who lives in Pasadena, immigrated here from China in 1986 and became a United States citizen in 1993. Earlier this year, she qualified for the U.S. Olympic team. Her trip to Atlanta will satisfy a career goal she once sacrificed when she retired from the sport in the mid-'80s. 15.7 inches.  By Dave Allen. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-BBO-WEAVER (Simi Valley, Calif.) _ To fully appreciate the meteoric rise of Jeff Weaver, you have to understand that just two years ago, he was an undistinguished, rarely used relief pitcher for Simi Valley High School's baseball team. He went off to college to study physical therapy. He enrolled at Fresno State and decided to try out for the Bulldogs' baseball team as a walk-on. Nobody knew who he was. Now he's an Olympian, making the team as a ``one-in-a-million longshot.'' 13.2 inches.  By Eric Sondheimer. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-RUN-JWILLIAMS (San Fernando, Calif.) _ For years, Jeff Williams of San Fernando was among the nation's best 10 or 15 competitors in both the 100 and 200 meters. And then, last year, he blossomed. At the relatively advanced age of 29, he ran personal bests of 10.19 in the 100 and 20.18 in the 200. He capped his splendid year by placing third in the 200 in the world championships. 14 inches.  By Jon Wilner. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-HKY-SWILLIAMS (Newbury Park, Calif.) _ Scott Williams vividly remembers the first time he ever heard of field hockey. It was the year before the 1984 Olympics in Los Angeles and members of the U.S. Olympic team were giving 12-year-old Williams and his classmates a demonstration of their game. Mike Newton and other '84 Olympians had shown up that day at Newbury Park's Sequoia Junior High School to drum up interest for a fledgling youth program. The sport already enjoyed growing enthusiasm among adults in Ventura County. ``They told us if we liked it to come out Saturday and sign up and they'd show us how to play,'' said Williams, who has learned the game better than anyone might have predicted. Thirteen years later, he is still playing _ as a teammate of Newton on the U.S. Olympic squad that will compete this month in the Atlanta Games. 16.2 inches.  By Dave Shelburne. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file). </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_19960714.0147 type=multi > <DATELINE>  (AM-PAGE1-CONSIDER-NYT) </DATELINE> <TEXT> (HLW)  EDITORS:  New York Times editors are CONSIDERING these stories for Monday's Page 1:  INTERNATIONAL  CENTRAL-EUROPE-WEAPONS (Budapest) _ The close of the Cold War ended the arms race between the superpowers, but ushered in an equally intense rivalry among arms manufacturers bidding for a share of the world's shrinking defense budgets. Prime target: Central Europe. Raymond Bonner.  NIRELAND-UNREST (London) _ A bomb ravaged a rural hotel in Northern Ireland, injuring two people and raising fears of a return to terrorist violence. The IRA denied responsibility. John Darnton.  UKRAINE-FARMERS (Bashtanka, Ukraine) _ Private farmers complain they are shut out from receiving any of the 600 American combines that are arriving in Ukraine. Who is getting the machines is a state-controlled company that is reselling them to government-run agricultural centers _ a move some say undercuts U.S. efforts to promote reform. Jane Perlez.  NATIONAL  NYC-SOARING-RENTS (New York) _ Finding a rental apartment in Manhattan has become as complicated and even more costly than it was in the mid-to-late 1980s, when soaring rents and prices, sprouting condominium towers, and co-op conversions helped define the character of an economic boom. New York is not, by most measures, having another economic boom right now; and that's where the rent issue gets interesting. The smoke is back, but without the fire. Kirk Johnson.  NYC-HMO-WOES (New York) _ Millions of New Yorkers have recently enrolled in HMOs, and most have slowly learned to navigate the system. But a growing number, mostly with chronic conditions or serious diseases, say they are being denied desperately-needed care. And instead of battling their illness, they find themselves fighting with their health plans for permission to get treatment.  FINANCIAL  JAPAN-STRONGDOLLAR (Undated) _ For a decade after the dollar began its long slide in value against the yen in 1985, American auto makers and other manufacturers enjoyed a special form of protection from Japanese competitors, whose imported products became almost inexorably more expensive in the United States. But now the sheltered years are coming to an end. The dollar has soared 39 percent from its trough in April of last year. As each dollar of sales in the United States translates into more yen at home, Japanese manufacturers have found themselves in the catbird seat, with a choice of grabbing bigger profits, cutting prices to build market share, or both. What it adds up to, a growing contingent of corporate leaders and economists say, is a new competitive threat to American companies, particularly auto makers. Keith Bradsher and Andrew Pollack. OLY-RUN-BOLDON (Los Angeles) _ You may not have heard of him, but Ato Boldon is a star athlete. At least in his native country, Trinidad, for whom the UCLA senior will compete in the Olympic games. OLY-SWM-BRIDGEWATER (Los Angeles) _ The Olympic experience is something Brad Bridgewater has been anticipating for four years now. As an 18-year-old freshman at the University of Texas, he was invited to the 1992 Olympic Trials. ``I was frightened by the whole thing,'' said Bridgewater, a two-time Florida state swimming champion. ``I was just a kid. It was pretty incredible. I was swimming with the best in the country. I was pretty satisfied with it.'' Four years changed Bridgewater, who swallowed the tough experience of finishing seventh in the 200-meter backstroke in 1992 and used it for inspiration for this year's trials. This time, the 6-foot-4 Bridgewater, now from USC, blew the field away and landed a berth in the 200-meter backstroke. 14.4 inches.  By Gary Washburn. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-HKY-BUTCHER (Simi Valley, Calif.) _ Nick Butcher of Simi Valley is, at age 20, the youngest member of the U.S. Olympic field hockey team. Nick is being groomed for international greatness down the line but he can contribute now. 15 inches.  By Eric Noland. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-VBL-CASTRO (La Crescenta, Calif.) _ After Gail Castro and her partner, Deb Richardson, surprised the beach-volleyball world by fighting through the trials in Baltimore and making the Olympics, they were called before the TV cameras for the customary quick interview. It was Castro's chance to say, ``Hey, a 38-year-old wife and mom has just defied the odds and made it to Atlanta!'' Or, ``Dreams do come true!'' Or maybe just, ``Hi, Larry! Hi, Larry Jr.!'' Instead, she opened her mouth and _ nothing. A chatty, friendly woman who had just showed guts and grit to help win a vital match, she froze as if the microphone were emitting tonsil-paralyzing gas. Partner Richardson had to do all the talking. Castro (nee Wolze) _ a former star at Crescenta Valley High, Valley College and Long Beach State, an ex-indoor pro in Italy, a pioneer of the women's pro-beach tour _ had all her struggles and disappointments and broken beach partnerships and sacrifices flash through her mind. 15.2 inches.  By Joe Jares. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-ROW-COLLINS (Thousand Oaks, Calif.) _ David Collins of Thousand Oaks has sacrificed plenty to compete in one of rowing's most grueling events _ lightweight fours without coxswain. ``When you get to the end (of a 2,000-meter, nearly six-minute race), I mean, your legs are burning, your lungs are screaming, and your vision starts to become a tunnel,'' the 26-year-old Westlake High graduate said. ``You focus on what's ahead _ the last 20 strokes, the person in front of you _ and almost let your body take over because your mind wants to tell you to stop.'' 15.9 inches.  By Kevin Modesti. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file). The 1980 Taft High School graduate is also one of the best hitters in the game. ``She's the person I would most like to have at bat with the game on the line,'' said Olympic team coach Ralph Weekly. OLY-SOC-FAWCETT (Los Angeles) _ Joy Fawcett did not play soccer because she hoped one day she would represent her country at the Olympics or the World Cup. That's because when Fawcett began, there was no women's soccer in the Olympics or World Cup. ``We were doing it because we loved the sport,'' Fawcett said. ``It's fun to see it grow.'' The 28-year-old Fawcett has seen many firsts for women's soccer. In addition to being a member of the U.S. National Team that won the first World Cup for women, she became the first women's soccer coach at UCLA. Now, she is competing in the first Olympics where women's soccer is a medal sport. 13.3 inches.  By Kathy Orton. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-HDB-FERCHO (Camarillo, Calif.) _ Denny Fercho plays team handball, a game not many Americans know. It's NOT like racquetball; rather, it resembles a combination of basketball, hockey, soccer, water polo and football. Fercho was exposed to the game first when he saw a match at the '84 Olympics in L.A. and, with the help of a grant of the Amateur Athletics Foundation, soon mastered it and became one of America's best players. Then he was cut from the team, told he was too small. He overcame that obstacle by mastering the fundamentals better than anyone. Says U.S. coach Rick Oleksyk: ``Denny is the smallest in size on the team, but possesses the largest heart. His desire is second to none.'' 14.8 inches.  By Kevin Modesti. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-SOF-FERNANDEZ (Los Angeles) _ If U.S. Olympic team pitcher/infielder Lisa Fernandez is not already the best softball player in the nation, just wait a while. ``God didn't give me the talent to run fast or be quick or be strong,'' she said, ``but He gave me determination.'' That quality has carried Fernandez from an inauspicious beginning to a level of play unmatched in either softball or baseball, in the opinion of UCLA coach and softball Hall of Famer Sharron Backus. ``Lisa Fernandez is the best all-around softball player in the history of the sport,'' said Backus, who coached Fernandez for four years at UCLA. ``If you try to compare her to a major-league baseball player, she has no equal. There is no one who can pitch, hit and field like Lisa in the major leagues, nor has there been an equal in collegiate softball.'' 14.3 inches.  By Dave Shelburne. OLY-ROW-FULLER (Westlake Village, Calif.) _ When the Fuller family first moved to the shores of Thousand Oaks' Lake Sherwood 20 years ago, one of the first gifts 8-year-old Amy received was a 12-foot wooden row boat. ``Her brother had a (sailboat),'' Diane Fuller, Amy's mother, remembered recently. ``She would get out there and challenge him to a race around the island in the middle of the lake.'' Thus began a sporting endeavor that brought Fuller a silver medal in the Barcelona Olympics in 1992, an accomplishment she hopes to improve on as her competitive life winds to a close this week in Atlanta. The odds are good that is precisely what will happen. Fuller and her world-champion American teammates are the favorites to win the women's eights class in Olympic rowing. 11.8 inches. OLY-RUN-GODINA (Los Angeles) _ After becoming the first American to qualify for the Olympic Games in both the shot put and discus since USC's Bud Howser in 1924, John Godina of UCLA still can't believe his resume, which includes the world title, five NCAA titles and a collegiate record of 72-2\ in the shot put. Godina now wants to strive for goals others have thought impossible. Foremost is becoming one of the best in the world in both throws. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-WTP-HACKETT (Los Angeles) _ If not for a protective mother, Dan Hackett might not be the No.2 goalie on the U.S. Olympic water polo team. He might not be the favorite to be the No.1 man in goal in Sydney in 2000. In fact, the UCLA graduate might not have ever even played the sport. ``My mom thought I'd break my neck playing football,'' said Hackett, who was not pleased with the decision at the time. ``Water polo is played during the same season _ so I went out for water polo.'' 13 inches.  By Dave Shelburne. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-VBL-HAGER (Los Angeles) _ Former Cal State Northridge star Axel Hager will be back on American soil this month representing Germany in the Summer Olympics in Atlanta. Hager helped lead the Matadors to their first NCAA championship appearance in 1992 in the Finals. Hager, who has qualified in beach volleyball, follows Bob Samuelson, who played for U.S. indoor team in 1992, as the second Northridge volleyball player to reach the Olympics. 12.5 inches.  By Tim Trepany. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-RUN-HANSON (Ventura, Calif.) _ Ventura's Sharon Hanson isn't convinced her troublesome right knee, which has required six different operations, hasn't helped her track and field career as much as it has hindered it. It was, after all, her first knee injury back in high school that kept a promising hurdler and long jumper from competing in her specialties and forced her to try a few new events during track season. Hanson discovered she could do those other things pretty well, so well that she'll be doing them against some of the world's best athletes in the Olympic Games' heptathlon competition in Atlanta. 12.7 inches.  By Tim McClain. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-CAN-HARPER (Ventura, Calif.) _ Ventura canoeist Joseph Harper is the sole American entry in the 1,000-meter single canoe sprint. It's been since 1952 that an American has medaled in a canoe sprint, and the 30-year-old paddler isn't expected to change that. To a certain degree that has worked to his advantage. He has already completed a lifelong dream of making it to the Olympics; everything else is extra. It may be the attitude he needs to reach his ultimate goal: a personal best time during the Summer Games. 15.9 inches.  By Brett Pauly. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-SOC-HEJDUK (Los Angeles) _ Frankie Hejduk had an unusual way of relieving stress before a game while at UCLA. He didn't pace the floor of his dorm room, or dribble the ball endlessly. He drove to Zuma Beach and went surfing. His endeavors not only clear his mind, they have helped him become a better athlete. ``I think one of the plusses to my game is I can run all day long. I attribute that to surfing.'' The 21-year-old defender, in addition to being a member of the Olympic soccer team, also plays for the Tampa Bay Mutiny of Major League Soccer. 14.8 inches.  By Eric Noland. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-RUN-HERAZO (Los Angeles) _ Her sport is a walking contradiction, but there is universal agreement on Victoria Herazo's designation as a world-class athlete. It's just hard to get recognition when your Olympic event seems based on compromise, or at least, appears slightly confused. But the Castaic resident has gotten well past all the jokes reserved for racewalking, a sport that is a cross between walking and running, but strays from completely relying on either motion. A simple rule to remember is that racewalkers are not allowed to lift both feet off the ground simultaneously during a race. 18.5 inches.  By Scott Wolf. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-ARC-HUISH (Simi Valley, Calif.) _ Justin Huish of Simi Valley, with grungy hipness and athletic good looks, has become the cover boy of sorts of the U.S. archery team. If you drive down Broken Arrow Street in Simi, you might come across the 21-year-old. To practice for the Olympics _ when he's not at the national training center in San Diego _ he shoots at a target set up between the barbecue and the wind chimes in his parents' backhard. To reach the minimum international range of 30 meters, he backs up through the garage, down the driveway and onto the street named, quite coincidentally, Broken Arrow. Then he fires away _ through a narrow doorway and, sometimes, over a sleeping Akita named Bear. 14.3 inches.  By Kevin Modesti. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-BBO-JONES (Los Angeles) _ With all of his physical skills, Jacque Jones looks deep inside when asked about his true strength. The former USC baseball player and second-round draft pick of the Minnesota Twins is as strong mentally as he is physically. While coaches and scouts rave about his rare combination of speed and power, Jones concentrates on his mental game. The outfielder credits a key adjustment in his early years at USC for his success on the U.S. national team. ``At first, everything that went wrong, I got down on myself,'' Jones said from Houston, Texas. ``When I got to USC, Coach (Mike) Gillespie taught me to keep my head up and stay in the game. Ever since then, it's been my strength.'' 14.1 inches.  By Gary Washburn. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-RUN-KAWAR (La Crescenta, Calif.) _ At Crescenta Valley High School, Nada Kawar played basketball and, when she needed a physical education class, decided to give track and field a try. Ultimately, she developed into one of the state's top throwers and earned a scholarship to UCLA, where she has competed in the shot put and discus in the NCAA meet the past two years. She has twice earned All-American honors indoors. Kawar, a resident of La Crescenta since her parents brought the family to the United States in 1989, will represent Jordan in the Olympics. ``They got a little excited,'' said Kawar, one of the top weight throwers at UCLA. It's no wonder. Until Kawar came along, she said, the national record in the shot put ``was around 37 feet.'' Now, her best mark of 55 feet, } inches has been certified as the best ever in the country's modest track and field program. (She has thrown 183-11 in the discus). Suddenly, Jordan, where she already has become something of a national figure, had an American-trained, world-class weight thrower. 15.2 inches.  By Michael Rosenthal. So when she competes in this month's Olympic Games, her fiercest opponent may well be the heat. Her confidant, guru and husband, Bob Kersee, hopes the humidity will help temper her asthma. With Florence Griffith-Joyner, her sister-in-law, officially out ofthe Atlanta Games, Jackie Joyner-Kersee may have the headlines to herself. She doesn't care. That isn't why she competes. ``She absolutely loves the sport,'' Bob Kersee explains. ``She's an athlete first and a celebrity second. 15.8 inches.  By Michael Ventre. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-RUN-KISS (Los Angeles) _ A college coach would be wise to always read his mail. Former USC track coach Jim Bush did. And that is how four-time NCAA champion hammer-thrower Balazs Kiss became a Trojan. A year out of high school, the 19-year-old Hungarian wrote to USC coaches in 1992 and asked for a scholarship. They had never heard of him. Four years later, Kiss has just finished a USC career in which he won every meet he entered. And he will represent his homeland in the Atlanta Games as a favorite to win a medal. 15.5 inches.  By Bill Schlotter. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-SHO-LAKATOS (Los Angeles) _ Josh Lakatos shoots trap for the U.S. Olympic team, a sport in which athletes fire at 200 clay targets traveling at nearly 80 mph from different stations over the course of several hours. A prodigy in the sport, the 23-year-old owns several world records. He recently won a match in Europe against essentially the same competitors he will face in Atlanta. Asked if he believes he will win the gold, Lakatos answers resolutely. ``Absolutely,'' he said, ``because I'm ready. There is no doubt.'' 17.2 inches.  By Michael A. Anastasi. The U.S. Olympian didn't take up the sport until age 12, when her cousin encouraged her to shoot around at a local gym. Slowly, she began mastering the fundamentals, and a year later she received her first college recruiting letter. 12.3 inches.  By Gary Washburn. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-RUN-MARSH (Los Angeles) _ Michael Marsh, a graduate of Hawthorne High School and UCLA, has quietly become one of America's best short sprinters _ 100 and 200 _ and, with two Olympic gold medals to his name, one of the nation's most accomplished. OLY-HKY-MARUQUIN (Ventura, Calif.) _ Ben Maruquin, starting sweeper for the American Olympic field hockey team, has not _ repeat, has not _ had a field hockey stick in his hands since birth. But pretty darn close. Actually, Maruquin, of Ventura, was about 4 when his father, Mogie Maruquin, joined the U.S. national team in 1974. His uncle, John Stockdill, was also a national team player. So a sport that few other Americans can claim even to understand came naturally to the 26-year-old seven-year starter. 13.1 inches.  By Bill Schlotter. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-RUN-MILLER (Los Angeles) _ Sprinter Inger Miller always had the genes. It was good health that had evaded her. Miller, a graduate of USC, is the daughter of Lennox Miller, the sprinter from Jamaica who won a silver medal in the 100-meter dash in the 1968 Olympics and a bronze in the same event in 1972. The first time she stepped on the track, as a child, she displayed raw speed handed down by her father, speed that enabled to succeed at Muir High School and USC. However, chronic injuries prevented her from taking that one final step to stardom _ until now. Miller, 24, outleaned Gwen Torrence to earn the third and final Olympic berth in the 200 and, as the fourth-place finisher in the 100, will be part of the 400 relay pool. 14.5 inches.  By Michael Rosenthal. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-HKY-O'NEILL (Newbury Park, Calif.) _ When the O'Neill family moved from Michigan to Newbury Park in the early 1980s, there was a bit of culture shock lying in wait for young son John. Ice hockey was his favorite sport. And they didn't play it in Southern California in those days. Then one of his new classmates at Newbury Park High School introduced him to field hockey. ``One of my friends said, `Hey, let's go try this out,''' O'Neill said. ``And I just kind of ran with it.'' Run with it, he did. All the way to the Olympic Games. O'Neill, Mr. Versatility on the American Olympic field hockey team, can play almost anywhere on the midfield or back lines, skills that have made him a valuable substitute. 13.4 inches.  By Bill Schlotter. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-BBO-PARQUE (Los Angeles) _ When UCLA left-handed pitcher Jim Parque was finally extended an invitation to try out for the U.S. Olympic baseball team last May, his reply was, ``What took you so long?'' ``I just knew all along I could compete with any of these guys,'' Parque said. ``I was a little disappointed with the late invitation, but an invite is an invite.'' Parque proved he deserved the tryout, because he made the elite 20-man team as a relief pitcher. The former Crescenta Valley High School star was considered a longshot at the start of training camp last June, but he showed he could be a left-handed setup man and spot starter. OLY-SOF-RICHARDSON (Los Angeles) _ Anything would be harder for Sherman Oaks' Dot Richardson than softball, a sport she has excelled at since age 10 _ when she was recruited by a women's Class A fastpitch team in Florida. Three years later, she was playing at the highest amateur level in the nation. By age 17, Richardson was competing against the best in the world as a member of her first of 18 consecutive U.S. national teams. Richardson later helped lead UCLA to the 1982 NCAA championship. Now she's an M.D., on one-year leave from USC Medical Center, competing in the Olympics for the first time. 17 inches.  By Dave Shelburne. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-WTP-ROUSSEAU (Los Angeles) _ Of the four basic old-world substances _ earth, air, fire and water _ Alex Rousseau finds three to be irrelevant. ``I'm definitely a water-oriented person,'' said Rousseau. ``Swimming, surfing, water polo ... I love the water.'' He also excels in it. Since his introduction to water polo as a freshman at Santa Monica High School, the former UCLA star, 28, has matured into a starter on the U.S. Olympic team. 12.8 inches.  By Bill Schlotter. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-SWIM-SALMEEN (Los Angeles) _ Annette Salmeen was a strong swimmer in high school, but she never thought she would make it to the Olympics. Her scientific approach to life _ she's a chemistry major at UCLA _ has helped her perfect her swimming, and she will be the first female Bruin since 1972 to compete in the Olympics, in the 200 butterfly and 800 free relay. ``Annette understands the difference between weight and buoyancy,'' says Bruins assistant coach Brad Burnham. ``She knows what a vector is. She understands the difference between speed and velocity and the weight of your hips versus the mass of your hips. Some swimmers go by what it feels like in the water. Annette needs numbers.'' To solve one of her recurring problems _ a slow-moving center of mass _ Salmeen watched underwater video and worked with her coach to figure out a way to position her body parts in the most effective way possible. ``To Annette, swimming fast is a problem that must be solved,'' Burnham said, ``and the pool is like her labratory.'' 14.7 inches.  By Jon Wilner. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-SOC-SNITKO (Los Angeles) _ Had be been composed of less-stern stuff, Chris Snitko might have wilted in the face of the legacy. Snitko more than held his own. He kept the line of succession strong by earning All-American recognition after the 1995 season, earned some distinction in the Bruins record book, and has capped the achievements with inclusion on the U.S. Olympic men's soccer team. 14 inches. But he will compete at the Summer Olympics in the 400 meters nevertheless. He will represent his father's homeland _ Antigua and Barbuda, the small island gems of the Carribean adorned with 365 beaches _ one for each day of the year. 14.1 inches.  By P.K. Daniel. OLY-GYM-UMPHREY (Los Angeles) _ It might be hard to find too many athletes at this year's Olympics who appreciate the opportunity more than gymnast Chainey Umphrey. Four years ago, at the Olympic Trials in Baltimore, the UCLA graduate felt like an Olympian for a teasingly brief period of time. As the gymnasts jockeyed for the seven qualifying spots, an assistant coach told Umphrey he was headed for Barcelona. By a small margin, the coach had made a huge mistake _ Umphrey missed qualifying by eight one-hundredths of a point. That was the last he wanted to hear about Barcelona. 17.4 inches.  By Brad Nelson. By Dave Shelburne. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-BOX-VARGAS (Oxnard, Calif.) _ One way or another, Fernando Vargas was going to fight. Even as an elementary school-aged child, he said, ``I loved to fight.'' And he did. In time, it was no longer news when Vargas was suspended from school, apparent evidence the boy was headed in a self-destructive direction. And then, when he was only 10, came a turning point. ``I was watching a local amateur boxing tournament on cable TV and was just mesmerized,'' said Vargas, a resident of Oxnard. ``I didn't know there was such a thing for kids. So all by myself, without my mom knowing, I found out where the gym was and went there. ``My mom didn't like it at all when she found out. She thought I would get hurt. When she saw I was hurting other people, though, I guess it was all right.'' 14.0 inches.  By Michael Rosenthal. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-TTN-WANG (Pasadena, Calif.) _ Once before, Wei Wang walked away from a career as a national-caliber table tennis player. By Jon Wilner. (Will move on the ``s'' sports file).  OLY-HKY-SWILLIAMS (Newbury Park, Calif.) _ Scott Williams vividly remembers the first time he ever heard of field hockey. It was the year before the 1984 Olympics in Los Angeles and members of the U.S. Olympic team were giving 12-year-old Williams and his classmates a demonstration of their game. Mike Newton and other '84 Olympians had shown up that day at Newbury Park's Sequoia Junior High School to drum up interest for a fledgling youth program. The sport already enjoyed growing enthusiasm among adults in Ventura County. ``They told us if we liked it to come out Saturday and sign up and they'd show us how to play,'' said Williams, who has learned the game better than anyone might have predicted. Thirteen years later, he is still playing _ as a teammate of Newton on the U.S. Olympic squad that will compete this month in the Atlanta Games. 16.2 inches.  By Dave Shelburne. "78129"	"I"	"mean"	" And he pointed out something that I have heard from countless other Chinese-American business leaders, which is that whether this large community of Chinese business people who are located in the United States, in Taiwan, in China, in Indonesia and a lot of other places around the world _ whether they basically identify more with Taiwan or with China in the differences between them, virtually 100 percent of them believe that the United States can do more for the cause of freedom and more for the cause of civility and more for the cause of prosperity by being engaged in China than by being isolated. . . . Q. Has he served as a go-between between you and the Indonesian Government or the Suharto Government, or with China? A. Not to my knowledge, no. I don't believe he has. You know, when _ I have conversations with hundreds of people, and I always assume that whatever I say to them they might say to someone else. Q. But you never asked him to undertake such a liaison? A. No, not to my knowledge. No, I mean, I did not ask him to do that. He has _ but if I said to him _ let me give you an example. I want to just _ if I said to him, I want a good, strong, positive relationship with China, and my objective is to engage China, not to isolate it _ which is something I have said to many people, mostly American citizens who do business in China, knowing that they might have contacts with people in the Chinese Government _ it would be clear to him and everyone else _ or if I said to him, I want to continue to pursue this relationship and make it ever better, I mean, it wouldn't surprise me if he said that to somebody the next time he was in China because I expect that to be done by _ I mean, a lot of times I do that deliberately knowing that it would be communicated. The Crown Prince Alexander, who has lived all his life in exile in London, has said he wants to return to live in Belgrade and will apply to have the royal residence, the White Palace, returned to his family. It was used by Slobodan Milosevic as his working residence until his ouster as president.  BRITAIN: FOOT-AND-MOUTH ALERT  Investigators continued trying to trace the complicated path of the outbreak of foot-and-mouth disease, as animals on five more farms were confirmed to have the disease, bringing the total of affected farms to 17. Seeking to stop the highly contagious virus from spreading further, the authorities canceled all horse racing in England, closed more national parks, and gave local governments the right to declare land off-limits to hikers and others.  NORTHERN IRELAND: TALKS ON ULSTER  Prime Minister Tony Blair and his Irish counterpart, Bertie Ahern, meet at 10 Downing St. on Wednesday to try to resolve differences between Catholic and Protestant parties over a start to disarmament by the Irish Republican Army, cuts in Britain's troops in the province and creation of a neutral police force. Six weeks of daily talks have failed to end the stalemate threatening Ulster's power-sharing government, and party positions are hardening in anticipation of a national election expected in May.  GERMANY: ANTI-HATE PROGRAM  Germany announced a $30 million program aimed at combating hostility to foreigners and anti-Semitism among young people. Christine Bergmann, the minister for youth affairs, said the program would help fund counseling centers for victims of far-right violence and help youths who want to leave skinhead and neo-Nazi groups.  ITALY: AIMING AT BERLUSCONI  With April elections approaching, the Senate passed a conflict of interest bill to rein in Silvio Berlusconi, the billionaire center-right candidate for prime minister who owns Italy's three largest private networks. The measure would oblige a high officeholder either to sell his holdings or surrender control to a blind trust, but it has to be approved by the lower house before Parliament disbands next week.  SPAIN: ANTI-NATIONALIST BASQUE  The hard-line interior minister who orchestrated a police crackdown on the Basque separatist group ETA, Jaime Mayor Oreja, has quit to run as the governing Popular Party candidate for the Basque regional presidency. Oreja, a Basque, will lead the center-right offensive in the May 13 elections against the Basque Nationalist Party, arguing that only a change of government can bring peace.  ELSEWHERE AROUND THE WORLD  MEXICO: FOX PARTY VICTORY CONFIRMED  A federal election tribunal confirmed that President Vicente Fox's political allies won November's gubernatorial election in Jalisco state. The candidate of Fox's National Action Party won the election cleanly, the tribunal ruled as it rejected the old governing party's charges of fraud. The Institutional Revolutionary Party, which lost the presidency for the first time in seven decades when Fox was elected last year, called the decision ``irregular and illegal.''  INDIA: PAKISTAN CRITICIZED  Home Minister Lal Krishna Advani told Parliament that Pakistan had not reined in Lashkar-e-Taiba, an anti-India militant group, which has made bold attacks on Indian forces in Delhi and Kashmir since India declared a unilateral cease-fire in Kashmir three months ago. But Advani also said Pakistan had decreased the intensity of its firing across the line that divides the portions of Kashmir that it and India control.  SUDAN: CHILD SOLDIERS FLOWN OUT  More than 2,800 former child soldiers were flown from the front lines in southern Sudan in an operation directed by the United Nations Children's Fund. The children, 8 to 18, had either received military training with rebels opposed to the government or had fought in the nation's civil war, which has gone on for 17 years and is thus older than most of the children. They had been evacuated in secret since Friday. </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20010227.0264 type=advis > <DATELINE>  (BC-BUSH-BUDGET-ADVISORY-NYT) </DATELINE> <TEXT> The New York Times plans the following package of articles relating to President Bush's budget speech:  BUSH-BUDGET (Washington) _ Ledeall: President Bush addresses a joint session of Congress at 9 p.m. EST on the budget. Greenwood also deserve inclusion, but they won't all get in this time. My ballot (if I had one after 27 years covering the NFL) would read: Parcells, Guy, Monk, Allen, and Kelly, with a sentimental vote for John Stallworth. Hayes, Donnie Shell, Casper, and Bob Kuechenberg all deserve slots, too, but they'll have to wait. Please don't bring up Harry Carson's name again. Green Bay wide receiver Antonio Freeman conceded last week that there will be a major difference Sunday playing in St. Louis's domed field rather than at frozen Lambeau Field. ``The sound will be vibrating off the walls of that dome,'' he said. ``There's a difference between playing outside and playing inside and a difference playing on grass and playing on turf so, I mean, yeah, there's a difference.'' You don't have to tell that to Packers quarterback Brett Favre. He's 10-14 in domed stadiums, including 2-7 at the Metrodome against the Vikings. His problem, people say in Green Bay, is that the noise level makes it difficult for Favre to be heard calling audibles, which he does more than most quarterbacks. When he goes to improvise in such circumstances, interceptions often result . . . TOP  Lede story:  WAGE-STAGNATION (Undated) _ Although the recession has ended, the wages of more than 100 million workers are still stagnant, endangering the consumer spending that sustains the fragile recovery. The stagnation in total wages paid to the nation's employees outside of government is now a year old, according to newly revised government data, which paints a bleak picture of the economy. By Louis Uchitelle.  (Scheduled to move by 2 p.m. ET in ``a'' news file.)  Top center story:  HEALTHCARE-COSTS (Washington) _ Ten years after a health care crisis threw American politics into turmoil, many experts see another one on the horizon. The cost of health care, which had stabilized in the mid-1990s with the advent of managed care, is climbing rapidly again, putting new strains on employers, workers and government health programs. By Robin Toner and Sheryl Gay Stolberg.  With graphics.  (Scheduled to move by 3 p.m. ET in ``w'' news file.)  Off-lead story:  ISRAEL-SETTLEMENT (Jerusalem) _ Five years ago, Israel's decision to build a sprawling housing complex on a pine-covered hilltop of east Jerusalem temporarily brought peace negotiations with the Palestinians to a halt. The plan was condemned by the United Nations, criticized by the United States and bitterly protested by Palestinians and left-wing Israelis. But in Jerusalem, the extreme has a way of becoming as routine as a mortgage payment. Now the project, called Har Homa, is becoming a reality of stark white stone and glass brick, a suburban dream hewn from a tormented landscape. By James Bennet.  With photos.  (Scheduled to move by 3 p.m. ET in ``i'' news file.)  FOLD  LATAM-ECON (Rio de Janeiro, Brazil) _ Brazil and other Latin American governments have followed Washington down the free market path, only to find they are now losing control over their economies. The immediate consequences are most visible here in Brazil. By Larry Rohter.  (Scheduled to move by 3 p.m. ET in ``i'' news file.)  BOTTOM  BOSTON-CATHOLICS (Boston) _ Late last month, the Boston Archdiocese decided to reject donations from a charitable fund set up by a moderate lay group. The group had set up the fund because the church sexual-abuse scandal had prompted many parishioners to refuse to give to the archdiocese. The fund was a way for people to give to archdiocesan charitable programs while bypassing the archdiocese's financial control. But when Cardinal Bernard F. Law refused to accept what many Catholics saw as much-needed money on the grounds that the fund would undercut the archdiocese's role, he succeeded only in further alienating some of his flock. By Pam Belluck.  (Scheduled to move by 2 p.m. ET in ``a'' news file.)  NY-PHONE-CARDS (New York) _ Hui Jiang, an immigrant from Fujian Province in China, has ambitions. In 12 years in the United States, he has gone from selling phone cards on the street to distributing as many as 20,000 cards a day to his own network of immigrant retailers. Jiang has accomplished this despite knowing limited English. ``I'm going to go step by step to become a big company,'' he vowed. ``If I need people to speak English, I'll hire them.'' He is hardly the exception in the cutthroat world of prepaid phone cards. The business may be driven by the desire of homesick immigrants to stay in touch with the family they left behind. But it has also, improbably, become a career ladder for immigrants with little experience and much bravura. By Susan Sachs.  With map.  (Scheduled to move by 3 p.m. ET in ``a'' news file.)  INSIDE STORIES PROMOTED ON PAGE 1:  IRAQ-MINORITIES (Barda Qaraman, Iraq) _ Decree 199, a presidential order issued by Saddam Hussein, is Iraq's equivalent of ethnic cleansing. Creating an Arab majority on the great Mesopotamian plain north of Baghdad is not an innovation by Saddam, who, like all Iraqi leaders since the state's founding in 1921, is an Arab. But Saddam, especially since his defeat in the 1991 Gulf War, has accelerated efforts to drive minorities out, and bring Arabs in, to the region that sits atop some of the world's greatest oil reserves. By John F. Burns.  (Scheduled to move by 3 p.m. ET in ``i'' news file.)  JEHOVAHS-WITNESSES-ABUSE (Undated) _ William Bowen always considered himself a devout Jehovah's Witness. As an elder in his Kentucky congregation, he said he saw it as his duty to inform church officials that a fellow elder had abused a child. But when Bowen contacted the church's headquarters in Brooklyn, he says, he was rebuffed. By Laurie Goodstein.  (Scheduled to move by 3 p.m. ET in ``a'' news file.)  CHURCHES-CHARGECARDS (New York) _ For millions of parishioners across the country, there is a new way to give to their local church: charge it. By Sarah Kershaw.
