"134715"	"I"	"mean"	"  </P> </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20021223.0158 type=advis > <HEADLINE> Editor's note: Karla DeLuca writes weekly about East Texas stories </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> 12-23-02 16:43:00EST (BC-HOMEONRANGE-1224-COX) </DATELINE> <TEXT> Every December, magazines declare this to be the ``merriest, brightest Christmas ever,'' which always leads me to question.  Can we really top ourselves year after year after year?  Are all our Christmases really merry and bright?  Is it really such a crime if they're not?  Some of the most memorable Christmases in our family have been due to circumstances not conducive to providing brightness, let alone merriment.  But when my family recalls Christmas past, it isn't the perfectly-prepared dinner, the artfully arranged decorations or the plans that went off without a hitch that we remember.  It's the time the turkey's browning bag exploded, blowing the door off the oven.  It's the time Sharla opened up the Christmas presents, skated around the living room in her new skates, played with Darla's Barbie, and broke it, then wrapped them all back up.  It's the time Daddy threw the Christmas tree out the front door.  The Christmas tree stand had been leaking sugar water into the carpet where it was quietly mildewing. This was discovered after days of wondering where the ``funny smell'' was coming from.  Daddy tried to put the fully-decorated tree into another stand, but the tree wasn't cooperating. After an extended period of being scratched by a tree that had not received moisture in two weeks, receiving a continuous stream of unsolicited suggestions, warnings and ``constructive'' criticism, he resolved to set the Christmas tree free. Fly little tree, fly.  There was the Christmas I had the mumps. I was going to get to ride on a float with the rest of the Blue Birds in the Christmas parade. I had come close to having a nervous breakdown over my inability to construct a pompom from blue and red plastic. We were supposed to shake these as we waved to the admiring crowds. Mine didn't come out a pompom, all I had was raggedy strips of plastic.  I was crushed when The Henderson Daily News published a news article about the impending appearance of the Blue Birds in the Christmas parade and left out my name.  I wondered if I had been struck off due to my lack of pompom constructing skills. It was only after getting a job at a newspaper that I realized it wasn't anything personal. The names were listed alphabetically and ``Karla Whitehead'' made the story too long, so they just cut off my name.  And then I came down with the mumps. My one chance to be in a parade, and it was going to pass me by. It wasn't even going to do that. I was sick. I was contagious. I couldn't even watch the parade and cheer on my fine-feathered friends.  But I did get to watch the parade. Daddy, a mechanic at the Buick dealership, which was on the parade route, parked a brand new Buick in front so I could watch from the back seat.  Daddy caught candy, and a record for me from the KGRI-Radio float. My sisters and I played the 45 of ``Shell Happy'' and ``Asia Minor'' for decades. The only possible connection ``Shell Happy'' might have with Christmas is that it does make you think of stockings and unwrapping. Like a Candy Barr.  But the Christmas memory that stands out most, the one that is repeated year, after year, was created by my brother-in-law, Dave.  It had been an especially memorable Christmas, involving in-laws, out-laws and spouses, old and new.  The special casserole my sister brought was sitting on the stove top, which someone accidentally turned on. The casserole dish broke, freeing three pounds of potatoes, sour cream and extra sharp Cheddar cheese to ooze down into the depths of the stove.  My sisters and I had shopped, wrapped, cooked and carted food, gifts, kids and husbands all over East Texas and North Louisiana in the quest of a perfect Christmas, and it was unraveling before our eyes like a cheap mitten.  We had suggested to the husbands that washing the dishes might be their token gesture of helping this year instead of sitting in front of the television to ``stay out of our way'' like they usually did.  And then it happened. Dave stood at the top of the stairs and uttered the words that will never die. ``We didn't come here to work.''  No sooner had the words left his lips did he wish, more than anything in his entire life, that he could call them back. But he couldn't.  ``You didn't come here to work? YOU didn't come here to work? YOU didn't come here to WORK?''  These words never left our mouths. We didn't say anything _ we didn't have to _ he knew exactly what we thought.  ``Uh, I mean, we had better get to work,'' he said, slinking into the kitchen.  That's right. Make sure those pots are the brightest ever. And you'd better be merry about it.  Hash Brown Potato Casserole  2 pounds frozen hash browns  1 can cream of mushroom soup  1/2 cup chopped onion  1 pint sour cream  2 teaspoon salt  1 teaspoon black pepper  1/2 cup melted butter  2 cups grated Cheddar cheese  1 -2 cup crushed corn flakes  1/2 cup melted butter  Mix potatoes, cheese, sour cream, salt, onion, soup and pepper together and pour into a 2-quart casserole. Top with crushed corn flakes mixed with melted butter. bake at 350 degrees for one hour and 20 minutes.  (Karla DeLuca is managing editor of the Marshall News Messenger. Her e-mail address is kdeluca (at)coxnews.com)  Story Filed By Cox Newspapers  For Use By Clients of the New York Times News Service </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20021223.0159 type=story > <HEADLINE> Golden skater conquers a big new whirl </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> ATLANTA  (BC-TV-SARAH-HUGHES-1224-) </DATELINE> <TEXT> "	"nyt_eng_200212.gz"

Observations for this column may be sent to Metropolitan Diary, The New York Times, 229 West 43rd Street, New York, N.Y. 10036. For faster consideration, send them by fax to (212) 556-3717 or by e-mail to diarynytimes.com. Please include your name, address and daytime telephone number (required); upon request, names may be withheld in print. Submissions become the property of The Times and cannot be returned. They may be edited, and may be republished in all media.  DEAR DIARY:  On a recent Saturday evening, after dinner at an Asian-fusion restaurant, watermelon martinis at a hip Downtown bar, and a live jazz show, I thought to myself that I had just had a quintessential Sex and the City New York evening.  Hopping on the 6 train to head home, I was even more excited to get a seat. At the next stop, an attractive young woman of about 25 came on and stood in front of me. She was wearing an outfit that looked very designer. Thinking that this night could not be any more uniquely New York, I was slightly surprised to see the woman tearing frantically at a magazine page. As is my custom when I see strange occurrences on the subway, I tried to look down and not stare. Yet the sight of this beautiful and hip woman grappling with a magazine page on such a beautiful and hip night was too gripping.  As things turned from quaintly odd to bizarre, the woman took the shredded page and wrapped it around her wrist.  Again, I sought distraction elsewhere: The young girl in a bathing suit across from me guzzling a gallon of orange juice, the mariachi band playing loudly to my left and the teenagers selling candy for their school basketball team were not able to help me keep my eyes from this beguiling woman.  When I finally looked up, I had to stifle a laugh at what I realized had actually happened. The magazine page was a full-color advertisement for a Rolex watch. The frantic shredding was to leave only the watch. The wrapping around the wrist was an effort to model the expensive accessory.  It was the perfect uniquely New York ending to the perfect, uniquely New York night.  -- SETH BLINDER  DEAR DIARY:  When my sister came to visit me in Connecticut, we needed (of course) to spend a couple of days in New York City. We checked into a the Renaissance New York Hotel in Times Square and planned an excursion for Broadway tickets.  Rain had been threatening, and, sure enough, the downpour came just as we were leaving our hotel. My savvy sister is from Seattle, and was equipped with a jacket with a hood. I inquired at the reception desk if any loaner umbrellas were available. They were all gone, so I checked with the bellmen. Emmanuel, at that station, declared that his supply had also been depleted. When I spotted a rain-soaked umbrella in his area, I said, How about that one?  No, he said, that is for our use. I said he didn't need an umbrella, he had a nice big hat. He then disappeared into his storeroom and returned with a bellman's hat for me to wear out to the theater. My sis and I set off in style, protected from the evening's showers.  -- KATHLEEN EVANS  DEAR DIARY:  The scene: Zabar's cafe. Me seated next to an early 20-something male. He was talking on a cellphone, perhaps to his girlfriend. I was (unavoidably) eavesdropping.  From what I was able to gather, they had gone to a party the night before where he drank too much. He explained his regrettable performance on the dance floor with the statement: It wasn't just the alcohol talking. It was the alcohol dancing.  -- MICHAEL MISCIONE  DEAR DIARY:  I spotted a middle-aged man on a 23rd Street crosstown bus firmly holding (but not crushing) an empty paper coffee cup with the Starbucks logo, upside down. Soon we both got off the bus and boarded another going north on Third Avenue, and this man now sat opposite me, still holding his cup.  I wondered what use he would put it to: holding rubber bands, pins or paper clips, soil for a tiny plant, or whether he just needed another paper cup at home. My curiosity forced me to get up and confront this total stranger. Excuse me, I said, but can I ask you why you have carried this empty paper coffee cup from one bus to another? What plans do you have for it?  He smiled and said, Well, I just don't want to throw an empty paper coffee cup on the sidewalk, and I haven't as yet found a trash bucket.  -- ALFRED GESCHEIDT  DEAR DIARY:  I was riding the M15 bus south down Second Avenue one recent Sunday, adjusting to the vagaries of the driver's personality. Ours today was splendidly aggressive yet skillful, with sure moves and a deft touch on the brake.  After the bus squealed to a stop at 26th Street, the driver opened the passenger door and gruffly yelled a few words before I could distinguish the tail end: Hey, get on the bus, you hear! A gray-haired, grandmotherly woman clambered up in the mild confusion, her face a bit dazed, it seemed, and slowly, slowly bent toward the driver, finally planting a tender kiss on his cheek. Then she shyly took a seat.  I wondered if this was the new way to avoid paying a bus fare. But a moment later, the driver, muscling his way into traffic again, shouted to his passengers, That lady used to change my diapers when I was a baby!  Upon disembarking, the old woman patted the driver's shoulder, and he took her face between his large hands and kissed her, lips to lips.  -- FIONA BAYLY  DEAR DIARY:  A few of my officemates and I decided to take a colleague, Elliot, to lunch in Manhattan for his birthday. We took a subway from our office in Downtown Brooklyn to our Midtown destination. After a few stops on the train, a man with a guitar got on and played an old country song. We applauded, gave him a dollar and asked him to sing Happy Birthday to Elliot, but the man told us he had to get off at the next stop, which he did.  After lunch, we made our way back to work by train. A few stops into our trip, the man with a guitar stepped into our car. He looked at us, then at Elliot, and started singing Happy Birthday. We sang along and the whole car applauded. Then he was off and on to the next train, to bring fun and surprise into the hearts of New Yorkers.  -- SHOSHANA SOROKA </TEXT> </DOC> <DOC id=NYT_ENG_20040704.0149 type=story > <HEADLINE> RESOLUTE FEDERER REBOUNDS AND REPEATS </HEADLINE> <DATELINE> WIMBLEDON, England </DATELINE> <TEXT> 	nyt_eng_200407.gz

, he figures,	 Wagner showed me on a series of maps how the process might play out in the New York State Senate. As each rural upstate district lost its prisoner population, to regain sufficient inhabitants it would have to reach into the next district, which in turn would have to reach into its neighbor. Wagner calls this effect the cascade. At the same time, urban districts would cascade in the other direction as they became, on paper, more densely populated. Somewhere in the middle (probably, he figures, in the suburbs around New York City) the two waves would meet, shifting the balance between urban Democrats and suburban Republicans in competitive districts and, as a consequence, the whole legislature. As Wagner admits, prisoners aren't unique in being counted in the census but denied the vote -- children and immigrants suffer a similar fate. But prisoners' interests, he argues, tend to be especially divorced from those of their legislators. The weight of the prison population strengthens the very districts and legislators with the most stake in tough-on-crime policies that create and fill more prisons -- figures like Dale Volker (a favorite target of Wagner's), the Republican state senator from New York's 59th district who has helped stymie popular efforts to reform New York's stringent Rockefeller drug laws. Wagner goes so far as to invoke the Constitution's infamous three-fifths clause, which counted slaves as three-fifths of a person for the purpose of Congressional apportionment. (It) wasn't so much an insult to the slaves, Wagner says. They were already property, they weren't even legally people, they weren't going to vote. But crediting their numbers to slaveholders, the worst people in the world for them, made it that much worse. The South used that added power to protect slavery against the more populous North for 70 years. But the three-fifths clause mattered as much as it did because in 1860 the United States had 4 million slaves in a population of 32 million, a proportion that even the most draconian prison policy would take a while to reach. The effects Wagner is talking about are shifts at the margins. He can't be sure, for example, that any electoral effects of the cascade wouldn't be blunted by the creative gerrymandering of incumbents determined to keep their seats. And even if the Census agreed to change its policy, poor rural districts would likely still support prison expansion for the economic benefits they're thought to bring. According to Karen Mills, a Census Bureau analyst, there are no plans to change the prisoner residence rule. Inmates are counted, she pointed out, just like residents of nursing homes, college dormitories, long-term hospitals, and domestic military bases. Besides, she says, it would be a logistical nightmare for the Census Bureau to have to contact all of the state and federal prisons in all 50 states and find out where (their prisoners) were going to go after they got out, or where they were sentenced, or where their own home is. They may not even have a home. Wagner believes that it would be easiest if the census methodology were simply changed -- ideally before the 2008 dress rehearsal of the 2010 Census. But he maintains that the real fault lies with the state and federal agencies that use the data, and he thinks a court might agree with him. Many state constitutions -- including Massachusetts' -- define residency differently from the Census Bureau, not as where one happens to be, but where one chooses to be. While he's not working on any litigation, Wagner thinks a group of urban plaintiffs could conceivably use that definition to sue their legislators for drawing districts that unconstitutionally diluted urban votes. That, of course, would require that people start seeing the matter not as a prisoner problem but as their own. Wagner sees this shift already happening -- in rural prison counties, of all places. Recently, non-prison towns in New York's Greene and Franklin Counties, feeling shortchanged in county-wide apportionment calculations, got the county government to stop neighboring towns from counting their prisoners. As Wagner says, When (rural) citizens recognize that this hurts them they want it changed. Which is good, because it means that the number of people who see themselves losing their democratic right in some important way from the census is expanded from urban people to rural people who don't happen to live next to the prison. And the more people feel shortchanged by the census, the more Wagner feels he's doing his job. </P> 	nyt_eng_200409.gz
, I think,	 This would rival, I think, the Hunley submarine that was recovered a few years ago, he said. </P> 	nyt_eng_200409.gz

The United States is adding to its foreign debt at an annual rate of $665 billion, or $5,500 for every household in the country.  JUDGE TRIES TO STOP YUKOS AUCTION  A judge in Houston tried to temporarily halt Russia's planned auction of the most profitable unit of Yukos. It was not known whether Russia would abide by the decision.  SOUTHWEST AIR TOP BIDDER FOR ATA  Southwest Airlines bid $117 million for some of the assets of ATA Airlines, a rival low-fare carrier founded by J. George Mikelsons. ATA sought Chapter 11 bankruptcy protection on Oct. 26. If approved by the bankruptcy court, Southwest would gain six gates at Midway Airport in Chicago, provide financing for ATA, and take a 27.5 percent stake in ATA once it reorganizes.  A STRANGE COINCIDENCE FOR GRASSO  A headhunter for the judge who will preside over the trial of Richard A. Grasso, the former chairman of the New York Stock Exchange, sent a copy of the judge's resume to the stock exchange in late 2002, promoting him as a possible candidate for the board. But lawyers for Grasso told the judge that they would not file a motion asking him to step down.  BUSH ON SOCIAL SECURITY CHANGES  On the second day of a White House conference on economic issues, President Bush said that addressing the problems in Social Security would reassure the financial markets.  FIDELITY DISCIPLINES 16 TRADERS  Fidelity Investments disciplined 16 traders as part of an investigation into whether employees improperly accepted gifts and entertainment from brokers.  REGULATORS ADOPT OPTION RULES  Accounting regulators adopted rules requiring public companies to treat stock options given to employees as business expenses.  GOLDMAN SACHS PROFIT CLIMBS 23 PERCENT  Shares of Goldman Sachs fell after Goldman Sachs reported a 23 percent rise in fourth-quarter earnings, driven by robust gains in investment banking, asset management and trading. But the results showed some weakness in the quarter when compared with the third quarter, including a decline in financial advisory fees, equity and debt underwriting, and a 22 percent drop in fixed income, currencies and commodities. The firm reported revenue of $4.58 billion for the quarter, slightly lower than Wall Street expectations for $4.8 billion.  OFFICIALS REVISIT FLU VACCINE RULES  Two months after the government recommended that scarce flu shots be reserved for people most at risk, health officials are worried that tens of thousands of doses of the vaccine could go to waste, and they are considering easing the restrictions.  DOW RISES; NASDAQ AND S&amp;P DROP  Stocks were mixed. The Dow Jones industrial average rose 14.19 points, to 10,705.64. The Standard &amp; Poor's 500-stock index declined 2.51 points, to 1,203.21, while the Nasdaq composite index lost 16.40 points, to 2,146.15.  UNEMPLOYMENT BENEFIT CLAIMS FALL  First-time claims for unemployment benefits dropped to 317,000; economists had expected a decline to 340,000.
